src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/7k6eJWUwesY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Nov 2020 17:39:51 +0000","feedId":26,"bgimg":"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/09/us-2020-election-footer.png","linkMd5":"5fa909b48327d685e1f918fddbb50b54","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn14@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-00-260_f68d83017a52e30b.webp","destWidth":3200,"destHeight":440,"sourceBytes":86137,"destBytes":51522,"author":"Taylor Hatmaker","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/09/us-2020-election-footer.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn14@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-00-260_f68d83017a52e30b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn18@2020_4/2020/11/12/04-50-03-573_cda007d67cf2c798.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn36@2020_4/2020/11/12/04-51-58-439_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=7k6eJWUwesY:HIsgQki_cQA:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn9@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-51-58-548_34f6689a0fddbd05.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=7k6eJWUwesY:HIsgQki_cQA:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn19@2020_2/2020/11/12/04-51-59-620_0078cbe9d771bbb6.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn3@2020_5/2020/11/12/04-51-59-581_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/7k6eJWUwesY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn7@2020_1/2020/11/12/04-51-59-481_de48ed3192a8d9e3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605156591258},{"createdTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:51","updatedTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:51","title":"RIP Google Music, one of the company’s last examples of generosity","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2072742","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/08/05/google-play-music-to-shut-down-starting-in-september-will-disappear-by-december/\">Google Music is dead</a>, and with it one of the few remaining connections I have to the company that doesn&#8217;t feel like a gun to my head. The service, now merged haphazardly with YouTube Music, recalled the early days of <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/google\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google, <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> when they sometimes just made cool internet things. It made it nearly a decade, though — pretty impressive for a one of their products.</p>\n<p>I&#8217;ll just say it up front: I&#8217;m a lifelong music pirate. Oh yes, I&#8217;ve reformed in recent years, but I&#8217;ve got a huge library of tracks that I&#8217;ve cultivated for decades and don&#8217;t plan to abandon any time soon (likewise you can pry Winamp from my cold, dead hands). So <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2011/11/16/google-music-opening-for-everyone-features-full-song-sharing-to-google/\">when Google announced back in 2011</a> I could stream it all to myself for free, it sounded too good to be true.</p>\n<p>And indeed it was a relic of the old Google, which was quite simply all about taking things that are difficult to do yourself (find things online, set up a new email address, collaborate on a spreadsheet) and make them easier.</p>\n<p>Google Music — as we&#8217;ll call it despite it having gone through several branding changes before the final indignity of being merged into another, worse service as a presumably short-lived tab — was not first to the music-streaming or downloading world by a long shot, but its promise of being able to upload your old music files and access them anywhere as if they were emails or documents was a surprisingly generous one.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-312269\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"997\" height=\"600\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg 997w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg?resize=150,90 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg?resize=300,181 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg?resize=768,462 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg?resize=680,409 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/06/google-music-beta.jpg?resize=50,30 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 997px) 100vw, 997px\" /></a></p>\n<p>Generous not just in that it was providing server space for 20,000 songs (!) for free and the infrastructure for serving those songs where you went, but in its acknowledgement of other models of owning media. It didn&#8217;t judge you for having 20,000 MP3s — they weren&#8217;t subjected to some kind of legitimacy check, and they didn&#8217;t report you to the RIAA for having them, though they certainly could have.</p>\n<p>No, Google Music&#8217;s free media locker was the company, or at least a quorum of the product team, announcing that <em>they get it</em>: not everyone does everything the same way, and not everyone is ready to embrace whatever business model tech companies decide makes sense. (Notably it has shifted several times more since then.)</p>\n<p>Though my perennial work frenemy at the time MG Siegler <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2011/05/10/google-music-beta/\">was not impressed</a> with the beta, <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2011/11/16/dont-be-too-disappointed-by-google-musics-lackluster-debut/\">I vigorously defended it</a>, noting that Google was starting simple and looking forward rather then trying to beat Apple at their own game. Plus, secretly, I was feverishly uploading a hundred gigs of music I&#8217;d gotten from Audiogalaxy, Napster, and SoulSeek. Here, I thought, was a bridge between my antiquarian habits and the cutting edge of tech.</p>\n<p>Since then, like the resentfully loving owner of a junker, over the years I&#8217;ve been frustrated by Google Music in the ways that only one who truly relies on something can be. The app became essential to me even as its ever-changing and confusing interface confounded me. As Google&#8217;s media strategy and offerings fluctuated and blurred, my uploaded music sat there quietly, doing the same thing it did at launch: hosting my music files. Whatever it did in addition to that, it still let me access the glitchy, 128kbps version of The Bends I downloaded in 2001. I also had the security of knowing if my many drives died in a fire, I could at least recover my precious MP3s.</p>\n<p>Whether I&#8217;d ripped it myself, pirated it in college, bought it on Bandcamp, or got it from a code inside the vinyl I bought at a show, it worked on Google Music. It integrated all my music in a truly all-accepting cloud player, and for that reason, I loved it in spite of its flaws and total lack of hipness.</p>\n<p>Now, in deference to the explosion of YouTube&#8217;s popularity as a music platform — which more  than anything else really is due to a new type of laziness and platform agnosticism peculiar to the next generation — Google Music exists as a sort of ghost of itself within the YouTube Music app, itself an evolution of a couple other failed music strategies.</p>\n<p>Perhaps Google felt that the optics of obsoleting a service and cutting off millions of users from something useful and beloved were not worth risking — after doing exactly that with Reader (RIP).</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"mKNmjuOM8W\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2013/03/17/god-damn-it-google/\">God Damn It, Google</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;God Damn It, Google&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2013/03/17/god-damn-it-google/embed/#?secret=mKNmjuOM8W\" data-secret=\"mKNmjuOM8W\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>So (after culling the users who forgot they had accounts) they settled on the next best thing, which was making Google Music suck. Buried inside the new app, the music I uploaded has undergone a regression: intermingled and mixed, poorly organized, unable to be searched through, and at every occasion presented as the worse option, the uploaded library function seems to have been hidden away and hobbled.</p>\n<p>The ugly and reliable Music Manager, which has run in the background on my Windows PCs for years, is dead, and adding new music is done by manually dragging the files onto the YouTube Music tab. Complaining about having to move my fingers a few inches when I get a new album seems a bit pampered, so I&#8217;ll just say that it&#8217;s telling that Google chose to make the user do the work when the whole service was built around preventing exactly that kind of work from having to be done.</p>\n<p>I suppose I&#8217;m an exception to the usual Google and YouTube user, and as I&#8217;ve been careful to show the company for the last 20 years or so, there&#8217;s no money to be made from me. Yet as soon as I understood that Google was going to make it hard for me to do what I had been doing with them for a decade, I decided I was willing to pay for it. Now I pay Plex for a service Google decided was below them, and incidentally it&#8217;s <em>way</em> better. (Come to think of it, I started paying for Feedly after Google killed Reader, too.)</p>\n<p>In a way I&#8217;m thankful. The idea of divorcing myself entirely from Google&#8217;s ecosystem isn&#8217;t a realistic one for me, though I do it where I can (though having moved to iOS, the cure sometimes seems worse than the disease). One of the tattered bindings holding me to Google was the music thing. And while I do plan to take up a hundred gigabytes on one of their databases somewhere for as long as I possibly can, I&#8217;m glad the company admitted that what they were giving me didn&#8217;t make sense for them any more. It means one less reason that what Google has to give makes sense for me.</p>\n<p>Every service from Google now, especially with those new, bad logos, feels less like it&#8217;s offering a solution to a problem and more like it&#8217;s just another form of leverage for the company. We were spoiled by the old, weird Google that did things like Books because they could, throwing it in the teeth of the publishers, or Wave, an experiment in interactivity that in many ways is <em>still</em> ahead of its time. They did things because they hadn&#8217;t been done, and now they do things because they can&#8217;t let you leave.</p>\n<p>So, RIP Google Music. You were good while you lasted, but ultimately what you did best was show me that we deserved better, and we weren&#8217;t going to get it by waiting around for Google to return to its roots.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=bi37oSjQFDo:D8xKa81dRUc:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img 12:49:51","updatedTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:51","title":"Twitter brings its Stories feature, Fleets, to Japan","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2072830","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/twitter\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"twitter\">Twitter&#8217;s <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> own version of Stories, which <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/03/04/twitter-starts-testing-its-own-version-of-stories-called-fleets-which-disappear-after-24-hours/\">it calls &#8220;Fleets</a>,&#8221; have arrived in Japan. The new feature allows users to post ephemeral content that automatically disappears after 24 hours. Though Fleets previously launched in Brazil, <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/06/09/twitter-brings-fleets-its-version-of-stories-to-india/\">India</a>, Italy and South Korea, Japan is notably Twitter&#8217;s second largest market, with some <a href=\"https://www.slideshare.net/DataReportal/digital-2020-october-global-statshot-report-october-2020-v01\">estimated</a> 51.9 million users.</p>\n<p>It&#8217;s also second in terms of revenues, led by advertising. In Q3 2020, Japan generated $132.4 million in revenue, coming in second behind the U.S.&#8217;s $512.6 million.</p>\n<p>Twitter can be experimental when it comes to new features &#8212; it even once developed a new way to manage threads with a public <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/11/27/twitter-tests-new-conversation-features-from-twttr-prototype-rollout-planned-for-2020/\">prototype</a>, coded alongside user feedback. But not all the features it dabbles with make it to launch.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>However, the further expansion of Fleets to Japan signals Twitter&#8217;s interest in the product hasn&#8217;t diminished over time. It seems it&#8217;s now only a matter of time before Fleets arrive in Twitter&#8217;s largest market, the U.S.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n<p lang=\"ja\" dir=\"ltr\">新機能のFleets（フリート）が登場<img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f44b.png\" alt=\"👋\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n<p>Twitter上での新たな会話方法が誕生しました。 </p>\n<p>日本の利用者の皆さん、順次ご案内予定です！ <a href=\"https://t.co/QAYxDdRfnN\">pic.twitter.com/QAYxDdRfnN</a></p>\n<p>&#8212; Twitter Japan (@TwitterJP) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/TwitterJP/status/1326298669461639168?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">November 10, 2020</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><script async src=\"https://platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script></div>\n<p>That said, the U.S. may be the hardest market for Fleets to crack, as here, many users are concerned about how all social media apps are starting to look alike.</p>\n<p>Whatever feature becomes a breakout success on one platform soon finds its way to all the others. In the early days, we saw this trend with the &#8220;feed&#8221; format, modeled after Facebook&#8217;s News Feed. The Stories format, popularized by Snapchat, came next. And now apps like <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/09/23/instagrams-tiktok-clone-reels-updates-to-allow-longer-videos-easier-edits/\">Instagram</a> and <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/10/15/snapchat-launches-its-tiktok-rival-sounds-on-snapchat/\">Snapchat</a> are ripping off TikTok with their own short-form video features.</p>\n<p>The result is that apps are losing focus on what makes them unique.</p>\n<p>Twitter, for what it&#8217;s worth, has historically been slow to copy from other social networks. In fact, it&#8217;s one of the last to embrace Stories &#8212; a feature that&#8217;s <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/09/24/linkedin-launches-stories-plus-zoom-bluejeans-and-teams-video-integrations-as-part-of-wider-redesign/\">now even on LinkedIn,</a> of all places.</p>\n<p>Plus, in Twitter&#8217;s case, the Stories feature may end up serving a different purpose than on other networks.</p>\n<p>Instead of offering users a way to post content of lesser quality &#8212; posts that didn&#8217;t deserve a more prominent spot in the feed, that is &#8212; Fleets may encourage users who haven&#8217;t felt comfortable with the platform&#8217;s more public nature to begin posting for the first time. Or, at least, it could push users to increase their content output and engagement.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"buszlGuJma\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/09/24/linkedin-launches-stories-plus-zoom-bluejeans-and-teams-video-integrations-as-part-of-wider-redesign/\">LinkedIn launches Stories, plus Zoom, BlueJeans and Teams video integrations as part of wider redesign</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;LinkedIn launches Stories, plus Zoom, BlueJeans and Teams video integrations as part of wider redesign&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/09/24/linkedin-launches-stories-plus-zoom-bluejeans-and-teams-video-integrations-as-part-of-wider-redesign/embed/#?secret=buszlGuJma\" data-secret=\"buszlGuJma\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>Twitter&#8217;s Fleets work much like Stories on other platforms. With a tap on the &#8220;+&#8221; (plus) button, users can post text, photos, GIFs or videos. Meanwhile, viewers use gestures to navigate the Fleets posted by others. The Stories sit at the top of the app&#8217;s home screen, also like on other platforms.</p>\n<p>Twitter tells TechCrunch all users in Japan should have Fleets available on their accounts soon, but couldn&#8217;t share a time frame for a U.S. launch.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=-nZc68ToTPw:4uTSvKZOgfs:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=-nZc68ToTPw:4uTSvKZOgfs:7Q72WNTAKBA\"><img 12:49:51","updatedTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:51","title":"To own an AR future, Niantic wants to build a smarter map of the world","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2072643","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/nianticlabs\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"nianticlabs\">Niantic <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> is continuing to bet heavily on the idea that it knows where consumer computing is headed, namely toward augmented reality.</p>\n<p>The game development startup behind Pokémon GO has some good company with companies like <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/apple\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"apple\">Apple, <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/facebook\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"facebook\">Facebook <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> and Snap making similar bets, but stakes are high for the studio that hopes it can build an early advantage in foundational AR infrastructure and bring third-party developers onboard, edging out efforts from companies that are quite a bit larger.</p>\n<p>Niantic&#8217;s experiments are still being bankrolled by their 2016 first-party hit Pokémon GO, which <a href=\"https://sensortower.com/blog/pokemon-go-one-billion-revenue-2020\">SensorTower estimates</a> is having its best year ever in 2020. A report from the firm suggests that the title has pulled in more than $1 billion in revenue since the start of the year, a marked increase since 2019 that might be surprising, given the social effects of a pandemic. Those revenues have allowed Niantic to be one of the more active acquirers in the AR infrastructure space, buying up small buzzy AR startups like Escher Reality, Matrix Mill and, most recently, 6D.ai.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>That latest purchase in particular has acted as a signal for what the company&#8217;s next plans are for its augmented reality platform. 6D.ai was building cloud AR mapping software, with companies like <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/airbnb\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"airbnb\">Airbnb <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> among its early customers. The tech allowed users to quickly gather 3D information of a space just by holding up their phone to the world. Since the acquisition, Niantic has been integrating the tech into their developer platform and has been aiming to juice the technology with their own advances in semantic understanding so they can not only quickly gather what the geometry of a space looks like, but also peer into the context of what the objects are that make up that 3D mesh.</p>\n<p><iframe class='youtube-player' width='640' height='360' src='https://www.youtube.com/embed/oG08OzwNvis?version=3&#038;rel=1&#038;fs=1&#038;autohide=2&#038;showsearch=0&#038;showinfo=1&#038;iv_load_policy=1&#038;wmode=transparent' allowfullscreen='true' style='border:0;'></iframe></p>\n<p>&#8220;We ultimately have this vision that for an AR experience, everything has to come together for it to be really magical,&#8221; Joel Hesch, Niantic&#8217;s senior director of Engineering, told TechCrunch. &#8220;You want precise location information so that you can see content in the right location and experience things together with others who are in the same location. You want the geometric information for things like occlusion or physics interactions. And you want to know about what things mean from a semantic perspective so that your characters can interact with the world in an intelligible way.&#8221;</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"u3VtBm1VS2\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/03/31/niantic-acquires-ar-startup-6d-ai-as-the-game-creator-squares-up-against-apple-facebook/\">Niantic squares up against Apple and Facebook with acquisition of AR startup 6D.ai</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;Niantic squares up against Apple and Facebook with acquisition of AR startup 6D.ai&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/03/31/niantic-acquires-ar-startup-6d-ai-as-the-game-creator-squares-up-against-apple-facebook/embed/#?secret=u3VtBm1VS2\" data-secret=\"u3VtBm1VS2\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>While they&#8217;ve been building out the tech, they&#8217;ve also been pushing users to try it out. Niantic has been urging Pokémon GO players to <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/05/26/niantic-to-begin-collecting-3d-visual-data-from-pokemon-go-players/\">actively capture videos of certain landmarks and destinations</a>, visual data from which is fed back into bulking up models and improving experiences for subsequent users. As users gain access to more advanced tech like the lidar sensor inside the new iPhone 12 Pro, it&#8217;s likely that Niantic will gain access to more quality data themselves.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"YcsjKxF5Dk\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/05/26/niantic-to-begin-collecting-3d-visual-data-from-pokemon-go-players/\">Niantic to begin collecting 3D visual data from Pokémon GO players</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;Niantic to begin collecting 3D visual data from Pokémon GO players&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/05/26/niantic-to-begin-collecting-3d-visual-data-from-pokemon-go-players/embed/#?secret=YcsjKxF5Dk\" data-secret=\"YcsjKxF5Dk\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>The ultimate goal of this data collection, the startup says, is to build an ever-updating 3D map of the world. Their latest tech allows them to peer into this map and distinguish what types of objects and scenes are in these scans, distinguishing buildings from water from the sky. The real question is how useful all of this data will actually prove to be in practice, compared to more high-level geographic insights like the Google Maps API.</p>\n<p><iframe class='youtube-player' width='640' height='360' src='https://www.youtube.com/embed/CTBtb4R_O6s?version=3&#038;rel=1&#038;fs=1&#038;autohide=2&#038;showsearch=0&#038;showinfo=1&#038;iv_load_policy=1&#038;wmode=transparent' allowfullscreen='true' style='border:0;'></iframe></p>\n<p>Though the company has been talking about their Real World Platform since 2018, they&#8217;ve been slow to officially expand it as the enthusiasm behind phone-based AR has seemed to recede since Apple&#8217;s initial unveil of ARKit in 2017 prompted a groundswell of attention in the space. &#8220;We&#8217;ve primarily been focused on first-party games and applications, but we are very excited about extending the platform to be something that more people can use,&#8221; Hesch says.</p>\n<p>For Niantic and other companies that are bullish on an AR future, their best bet seems to be quietly building and hoping that their R&#38;D will give them a years-long advantage when the technology potentially starts landing more consumer hits.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"mlI4LWwt5C\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/03/29/the-niantic-ec-1/\">The Niantic builds","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2072850","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Electric automaker <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/rivian-automotive\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"rivian-automotive\">Rivian <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> will makes its hands-free driver assistance system standard in every vehicle it builds, including its first two vehicles — the RT1 pickup truck and R1S SUV — that are coming to market in 2021.</p>\n<p>Details about the system, which is branded as Driver+, was just one of <a href=\"https://stories.rivian.com/time-to-build\">numerous new bits of information</a> released Wednesday on its website, including prices and specs on its R1T pickup truck and R1S SUV.</p>\n<p>Rivian said the driver assistance system will automatically steer, adjust speed and change lanes on command. The capabilities of the system that Rivian describes suggests it is a Level 2 system as designated by SAE International. Level 2 means the system can perform two or more parts of the driving task under supervision of the driver. To support this level of driving, the system will be powered by two redundant compute platforms, 12 ultrasonic sensors, 10 exterior cameras, five radars and high-precision GPS. This essentially gives the vehicle 360-camera and radar visibility. It&#8217;s a robust suite of hardware that exceeds what Tesla uses for its driver assistance system. The hardware suite is similar to GM&#8217;s hands-free Super Cruise system, with the exception that Rivian appears to have more cameras.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"ZGqx4zCBoe\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/24/rivian-to-begin-deliveries-of-electric-pickup-truck-in-june-2021/\">Rivian to begin deliveries of electric pickup truck in June 2021</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;Rivian to begin deliveries of electric pickup truck in June 2021&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/24/rivian-to-begin-deliveries-of-electric-pickup-truck-in-june-2021/embed/#?secret=ZGqx4zCBoe\" data-secret=\"ZGqx4zCBoe\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>Rivian is also placing a driver-monitoring system that includes a cabin-facing camera in its vehicles to ensure that drivers keep their eyes on the road when the system is engaged. Initially, the hands-free system will only be available on select highways and will then expand over time — improvements achieved via over-the-air software updates — to include a broader geographic area and more road types. This is similar to GM&#8217;s approach with its hands-free Super Cruise system, which was initially limited to certain divided highways and eventually expanded.</p>\n<p>While there are a number of automakers with Level 2 systems, they vary in capability. GM&#8217;s hands-free Super Cruise and Tesla&#8217;s Autopilot systems are considered some of the most capable and easy to use, per a recent Consumer Reports evaluation of driver assistance systems. However, Tesla&#8217;s system scored lower overall because it <a href=\"https://www.consumerreports.org/car-safety/cadillac-super-cruise-outperforms-other-active-driving-assistance-systems/\">lacks a driver monitoring system</a> that makes sure the driver is alert and paying attention to the road.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" storage","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2072629","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Come June 1, 2021, <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/google\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> will change its storage policies for free accounts &#8212; and not for the better. Basically, if you&#8217;re on a free account and a semi-regular Google Photos user, get ready to pay up next year and subscribe to <a href=\"https://one.google.com/\">Google One</a>.</p>\n<p>Currently, every free Google account comes with 15 GB of online storage for all your Gmail, Drive and Photos needs. Email and the files you store in Drive already counted against those 15 GB, but come June 1, all Docs, Sheets, Slides, Drawings, Forms or Jamboard files will count against the free storage as well. Those tend to be small files, but what&#8217;s maybe most important here, virtually all of your Photos uploads will now count against those 15 GB as well.</p>\n<p>That&#8217;s a big deal because today, Google Photos lets you store <a href=\"https://support.google.com/photos/answer/6220791?co=GENIE.Platform%3DAndroid&#38;hl=en\">unlimited images</a> (and unlimited video, if it&#8217;s in HD) for free as long as they are under 16MP in resolution or you opt to have Google degrade the quality. Come June of 2021, any new photo or video uploaded in high quality, which currently wouldn&#8217;t count against your allocation, will count against those free 15 GB.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2072641\" style=\"width: 409px\" class=\"wp-caption alignright\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Storage-management-tool.gif\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2072641\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\" wp-image-2072641\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Storage-management-tool.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"399\" height=\"399\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2072641\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> Google</p></div></p>\n<p>As people take more photos every year, that free allotment won&#8217;t last very long. Google argues that 80% of its users will have at least three years to reach those 15 GB. Given that you&#8217;re reading TechCrunch, though, chances are you&#8217;re in the 20% that will run out of space much faster (or you&#8217;re already on a Google One plan).</p>\n<p>Some good news: To make this transition a bit easier, photos and videos uploaded in high quality before June 1, 2021 will not count toward the 15 GB of free storage. As usual, original quality images will continue to count against it, though. And if you own a Pixel device, even after June 1, you can still upload an unlimited number of high-quality images from those.</p>\n<p>To let you see how long your current storage will last, Google will now show you personalized estimates, too, and come next June, the company will release a new free tool for Photos that lets you more easily manage your storage. It&#8217;ll also show you dark and blurry photos you may want to delete &#8212; but then, for a long time Google&#8217;s promise was you didn&#8217;t have to worry about storage (remember Google&#8217;s old Gmail motto? &#8220;Archive, don&#8217;t delete!&#8221;).</p>\n<p>In addition to these storage updates, there&#8217;s a few additional changes worth knowing about. If your account is inactive in Gmail, Drive or Photos for more than two years, Google &#8220;may&#8221; delete the content in that product. So if you use Gmail but don&#8217;t use Photos for two years because you use another service, Google may delete any old photos you had stored there. And if you stay over your storage limit for two years, Google &#8220;may delete your content across Gmail, Drive and Photos.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Cutting back a free and (in some cases) unlimited service is never a great move. Google argues that it needs to make these changes to &#8220;continue to provide everyone with a great storage experience and to keep pace with the growing demand.&#8221;</p>\n<p>People now upload more than 4.3 million GB to Gmail, Drive and Photos every day. That&#8217;s not cheap, I&#8217;m sure, but Google also controls every aspect of this and must have had some internal projections of how this would evolve when it first set those policies.</p>\n<p>To some degree, though, this was maybe to be expected. This isn&#8217;t the freewheeling Google of 2010 anymore, after all. We&#8217;ve already seen <a href=\"https://9to5google.com/2020/11/06/google-photos-editor-lock-features-google-one/\">some indications</a> that Google may reserve some advanced features for Google One subscribers in Photos, for example. This new move will obviously push more people to pay for Google One and more money from Google One means a little bit less dependence on advertising for the company.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=g5S-JJGPBxA:9C__LvyKQA8:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a staff","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2072905","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">President-elect Joseph Biden has plucked Ron Klain, a longtime colleague and confidant and the current executive vice president of the venture capital firm Revolution, as his White House chief of staff, reports The <a class=\"c-link\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/11/11/us/politics/ron-klain-biden.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-stringify-link=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/11/11/us/politics/ron-klain-biden.html\" data-sk=\"tooltip_parent\">New York Times. </a></p>\n<p>Klain was Biden’s chief of staff for two years during the Obama administration and <a class=\"c-link\" href=\"https://thecaucus.blogs.nytimes.com/2011/01/04/ron-klain-leaving-vice-presidents-staff\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-stringify-link=\"https://thecaucus.blogs.nytimes.com/2011/01/04/ron-klain-leaving-vice-presidents-staff\" data-sk=\"tooltip_parent\">left his post as chief of staff </a>in 2011 to join Revolution, the firm founded by former AOL chief executive and founder Steve Case. Revolution did not immediately respond to a request for comment.</p>\n<p>If Klain makes his second entrance into the White House, Biden will be bringing on a chief of staff he&#8217;s known for more than 35 years. The duo first worked together in 1989, when the president-elect was a senator and Klain was a newly graduated law student from Harvard Law School. He most recently worked as the White House Ebola Response coordinator from October 2014 to February 2015, and helped as a debate advisor to President Obama and President Clinton, as well as nominees Al Gore, John Kerry and Hillary Clinton.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>Klain&#8217;s appointment could pacify some of the presumed tension that could occur between startups and the government under the Biden-Harris administration. Biden has been vocal about pursuing aggressive regulation on the tech industry, which could negatively impact behemoths like Google, Apple and Facebook. Klain has spoken up (<a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2015/03/22/the-regulatory-fault-line-for-startups-moves-to-washington/\">in TechCrunch!</a>) about how regulatory hurdles could hinder key innovation in startup-land. Klain also helped lead efforts for <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/04/03/higher-ground-labs-2018-dnc-startups/\">Higher Ground Labs</a>, an incubator and accelerator focused on politically-focused (and Democrat-loved) startups. While that likely wouldn&#8217;t impact Big Tech, it doesn&#8217;t hurt that, reportedly, one of Biden&#8217;s closest confidants will have a soft spot for startups.</p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=QGNZXol7AdE:Le6NPAkemiI:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img This is your Daily Crunch for November 11, 2020.</p>\n<p><strong>The big story: Google Photos will end free, unlimited storage</strong></p>\n<p>Google is <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/11/11/google-cuts-back-on-its-free-storage-get-ready-to-pay-up/\">changing its storage policies</a> for free accounts in a way that could have a big impact on anyone regularly using Google Photos.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>Currently, Google Photos allows users to store unlimited images (and HD video) as long as they&#8217;re under 16 megapixels. Starting on June 1, 2021, new photos and videos will all count toward the 15 gigabytes of free storage that the company offers to anyone with a free Google account.</p>\n<p>Google says it will take the average user three years to reach 15 gigabytes — at which point they&#8217;ll either need to delete some photos or pay for a Google One account. Also on June 1: Docs, Sheets, Slides, Drawings, Forms and Jamboard files will start counting toward your storage total as well.</p>\n<p><strong>The tech giants</strong></p>\n<p><a
